  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8a1aeeea-3700-4363-8178-1bf68991f6a2'
      });
    }

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122162-lori?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122162-lori?shelf=2007september">2007september</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6468173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3761595034746161209">This may be the best collection of short stories I've ever read. It's undoubtably the most twisted. (And trust me. I read twisted.)<br/><br/>I've always been a huge fan of Dahl's kids' books. It's not a big leap, I suppose, to imagine Charlie all grown up and sick with chocolate power. And the climactic scene of Danny, the Champion of the World, is here nearly in its entirety. (I need to go back and see which came first. I'm guessing this one.)<br/><br/>As masterful as Dahl is with novels, he's perfected the short </span>
  <span id="freeText3761595034746161209" style="display:none">This may be the best collection of short stories I've ever read. It's undoubtably the most twisted. (And trust me. I read twisted.)<br /><br />I've always been a huge fan of Dahl's kids' books. It's not a big leap, I suppose, to imagine Charlie all grown up and sick with chocolate power. And the climactic scene of Danny, the Champion of the World, is here nearly in its entirety. (I need to go back and see which came first. I'm guessing this one.)<br /><br />As masterful as Dahl is with novels, he's perfected the short story as an art form. Even though you know the twist is coming ... and you're waiting ... it always catches you off guard and leaves you hanging, laughing, wincing as you turn the page and dive into the next. He's at once subtle and over the top. It's a remarkable balancing act.<br /><br />The stories are arranged chronologically, and I did for a bit think that Dahl either hated women or at least thought they were more capable of deviousness than men. As it continues, though, his male characters get in on the fun.<br /><br />My faves: The Visitor, The Great Switcheroo, Parson's Pleasure, Royal Jelly, Lamb to the Slaughter and William and Mary.<br /><br />Finding a new author is like falling in love. Finding a new branch of an old one? Falling in love with an old friend. It's even better.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3761595034746161209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer780921" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5459688190017593209">There is no darker, funnier writer than Roald Dahl. His children's stories were the original series of unfortunate events. (Charlie and the Chocolate Factory, James and the Giant Peach, etc.) In the 60's, Alfred Hitchcock had a television series, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2014794.The_Thirty_nine_Steps_' title='The Thirty-nine Steps. by John Buchan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alfred Hitchcock</a> presents, in which he used many of the stories found in this anthology.   <span id="freeText5459688190017593209" style="display:none">There is no darker, funnier writer than Roald Dahl. His children's stories were the original series of unfortunate events. (Charlie and the Chocolate Factory, James and the Giant Peach, etc.) In the 60's, Alfred Hitchcock had a television series, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2014794.The_Thirty_nine_Steps_" title="The Thirty-nine Steps. by John Buchan" rel="nofollow noopener">Alfred Hitchcock</a> presents, in which he used many of the stories found in this anthology. Also, one of his stories was made into a vignette in the movie Four Rooms. (Tarantino's contribution)<br /><br />Dahl is as creepy in his descriptions as any "horror" writer to date. There is an uneasy churning in the stomach of the reader upon finishing stories like Pig, The Rat Catcher, and the Bookseller. Like a brilliant comedian that holds you for ten minutes until a great punchline, Dahl's punch is straight to your gut.<br /><br />My personal favorite stories are The Visitor and The Wonderful Story of Henry Sugar. The journey is worth the shock. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5459688190017593209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11618685709400984958">Roald Dahl was the quintessential story teller of all time. His prose cuts to the essential element of his characters and story. Not to mention that his subject matter is always amazingly unique and interesting. It's sad to think that there will never be another new Dahl story. <br/><br/>This passage particularly struck me from his story Georgy Porgy and I share, "I spend many hours each day playing with sentences. I regard each sentence as a little wheel, and my ambition lately has been to gather several</span>
  <span id="freeText11618685709400984958" style="display:none">Roald Dahl was the quintessential story teller of all time. His prose cuts to the essential element of his characters and story. Not to mention that his subject matter is always amazingly unique and interesting. It's sad to think that there will never be another new Dahl story. <br /><br />This passage particularly struck me from his story Georgy Porgy and I share, "I spend many hours each day playing with sentences. I regard each sentence as a little wheel, and my ambition lately has been to gather several hundred of them together at once and to fit them all end to end, with the cogs interlocking, like gears, but with each wheel a different size,each turning at a different speed. Now and again I try to put a really big one right next to a very small one in such a way that the big one, turning slowly, will make the small one spin so fast that it hums. Very tricky, that."<br /><br />I especially love the stories 'The Way up to Heaven' and 'Skin'.<br /><br />If you haven't already, you must read this book!</span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/703687651?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnes" href="/user/show/17057547-agnes">Agnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer703687651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12125175738855266999">Why I decided to read this book<br/>This book provides a variety of short stories that are categorised as the 'best of Roald Dahl', and Roald Dahl has written such amazing books such as 'Matilda' and 'The Twits'. Roald Dahl was a big part of my childhood, personally, and many other children so I was instantly drawn to this book. The 3 short stories I chose to read from this anthology is 'Lamb to the slaughter', 'The way up to heaven' and 'The Landlady'. I chose these stories because they had intrigui</span>
  <span id="freeText12125175738855266999" style="display:none">Why I decided to read this book<br />This book provides a variety of short stories that are categorised as the 'best of Roald Dahl', and Roald Dahl has written such amazing books such as 'Matilda' and 'The Twits'. Roald Dahl was a big part of my childhood, personally, and many other children so I was instantly drawn to this book. The 3 short stories I chose to read from this anthology is 'Lamb to the slaughter', 'The way up to heaven' and 'The Landlady'. I chose these stories because they had intriguing and mysterious titles that drew me to reading it. I personally am very fond of thrillers and horror stories, so all these three texts were very captivating in the sense that they provided a fascinating ending.<br /><br />Which category on the Bingo Board this book completes:<br />THREE poems or short stories from ONE anthology<br />This category allows us to read a variety of texts written by one author to understand how they write; their style/structure, the purpose of their texts etc. Roald Dahl has written these short stories very cleverly as they are under-dramatised yet very powerful . These 3 short stories were very interesting as they had my attention throughout the whole story and almost gave you chills whenever you read the last page. So, when I was reading the last page of my third short story I expected to be stunned at the end, and no surprises, I was. <br /><br />My favourite quote from this book (and why) <br /><br />(Lamb to the slaughter): "Whoever done it, they're not going to be carrying a thing like that around with them longer than they need... Personally, I think it's right here on the premises... Probably right under our very noses. What do you think, Jack?'<br />This is my favourite quote(s) from this short story because Mrs Maloney (murderer of her husband, Patrick Maloney) acted very innocent as her husband's colleagues feasted on the very murder weapon they were enquiring about. <br /><br />(The Landlady) : “I stuff all my little pets myself when they pass away. Will you have another cup of tea?” This is my favourite quote from this short story because it informs the reader about how peculiar the landlady is, we can sense that the landlady is a dreadful person. This quote gives hints to the reader on how her next victim (Mr.Billy Weaver) is going to be 'taken care of'.<br /><br />(The way up to heaven) : “It is really extraordinary how in certain people a simple apprehension about a thing like catching a train can grow into a serious obsession.” This is my favourite quote because it sums up Mrs. Foster's personality which is something that I can definitely relate to. But, the difference is that Mrs. Foster's 'obsession' leads her to leaving her husband in a broken elevator while she was going to Paris for 6 weeks. Being on time for her plane certainly meant more to Mrs. Foster than her laid back husband. <br /><br />Something new I learned from this book is:<br />Overral, combining all three short stories, I learnt that you should not trust people based on your 30 seconds of a first impression because for all you know they could be a master mind killer. So, get to know people before really trusting them. This book provides various reasons as to why you should not trust strangers. Our parents usually tell us "don't talk to strangers" if you read these short stories, you will really understand why. I have also learned that sometimes in life we need to take a step back and relax because being overly paranoid can become 'obsessive'.<br /><br />A character or setting in this book that was interesting to me (and why):<br />(Lamb to the slaughter): My favourite character is Mrs Maloney. She came across as a very intriguing character. Her personality is very peculiar in the beginning as she seemed obsessed with being the perfect housewife. A bit overly friendly as she hides behind her cunning murder scheme of killing her husband. She shows no remorse to her actions being the "vulnerable" wife she was at the time, they didn't suspect a thing.<br /><br />(The landlady): My favourite character from this short story is the landlady herself. I find her the most interesting because of her personality; the way she composes herself even though she knows exactly what she is doing. <br /><br />(The way up to heaven) : My favourite character in this short story is Mrs. Foster. She is 'overly obsessed' with being on time to the point where her eye starts to twitch in paranoia. Mr. Eugene Foster (Mrs. Foster's husband) is the complete opposite of his wife, he would take his time and would not care if he or his wife are late. So, because Mr. Foster feels he needs to be ignorant to his wife's paranoia of being late, she gets to the point where she cannot take any more. She leaves him in the broken elevator in their home, and heads off to paris for 6 weeks.<br /><br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="12125175738855266999" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_353841642" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/353841642" />
    <a title="Stephanie A. Higa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/686879-stephanie-a-higa"><img alt="Stephanie A. Higa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1198902607p2/686879.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/353841642?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie A. Higa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie A. Higa" href="/user/show/686879-stephanie-a-higa">Stephanie A. Higa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer353841642" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10148538867085773328">It's true that nearly all writing reveals a great deal about the person behind the words, but it's especially true in Roald Dahl's case because all his stories have more or less the same tone: classy, stylish, and gruesome. This includes his children's stories, which are known for being playful and funny. Yes, they definitely are, but they're also kind of disturbing from an adult perspective. (<em>James and the Giant Peach</em>: orphan trapped by abusive aunts finds an escape in an oversized fruit occupi</span>
  <span id="freeText10148538867085773328" style="display:none">It's true that nearly all writing reveals a great deal about the person behind the words, but it's especially true in Roald Dahl's case because all his stories have more or less the same tone: classy, stylish, and gruesome. This includes his children's stories, which are known for being playful and funny. Yes, they definitely are, but they're also kind of disturbing from an adult perspective. (<em>James and the Giant Peach</em>: orphan trapped by abusive aunts finds an escape in an oversized fruit occupied six anthropomorphic insects. <em>Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</em>: nice poor kid wins a tour to a famous chocolate factory that employs green/orange workers who seem to have that "collective unconscious" thing going on, and is run by a maniac who concocts and executes special punishments--totally justified but also likely in violation of Department of Health standards--for the stupid, abominable children accompanying said nice kid on the tour. <em>The Twits</em>: an evil, disgusting married couple who abuse each other and the animals in their backyard get their comeuppance from the animals. <em>The Witches</em>: apparently there exist some women with malformed feet who prey on young children and transform them into mice.)<br /><br />The stories in this volume are for adults, though, so they're not exactly the same as his children's offerings. Instead of wacky humor, Dahl gives us sleek twists. Instead of good little Charlie Bucket, he gives us three-dimensional nightmares, like glutton Augustus Gloop, all grown up, and Willy Wonka, if he hadn't dedicated his life to chocolate. (Willy Wonka IS a psychopath, okay. Don't listen to the movies.) With the exception of "The Wonderful Story of Henry Sugar" (which, along with "The Hitchhiker," was deemed appropriate for the children's collection <em>The Wonderful Story of Henry Sugar and Six More</em>) and "William and Mary," these stories are all realistic. That is: they're realistic in the sense that they could happen, but only with the help of unusually deranged and creative people. As in good science fiction, Dahl makes the highly improbable seem highly possible. His characters are out of this world, but their thoughts and actions adhere to a very human logic. That's what makes them fascinating and believable (although not what I'd call sympathetic).<br /><br />Roald Dahl is a damn good writer. That clearly comes out in his character development, but even more so in his plots. He's a master storyteller. He doesn't rely on frills and backflips to get you to like him; he just <em>is</em>. His endings are surprising and unpredictable, but they also flow naturally from the actions leading up to them. His plots are excellent, with just the right amount of pacing and no excess showy nonsense. Dahl's stories read as if he's lived each of them himself, or at the very least thought them through a hundred times over. It's almost disturbing. (You know, come to think of it, Roald Dahl probably was Uncle Oswald with the brains of Willy Wonka. Just look at photos of him.)<br /><br />My only beef with this volume is the chunk of "Claud's Dog" stories near the end. These were so boring that I kept looking for the catch. Are they told backwards? Is there some huge reveal about Gordon at the end? Why is Claud's dog hardly even present for most of these stories? Alas, nothing.<br /><br />Highlights:<br />"Lamb to the Slaughter"<br />"Genesis and Catastrophe"<br />"The Visitor"<br />"The Great Switcheroo"<br />"The Hitchhiker" (so damn good I remembered it almost in its entirety from childhood)<br />"The Wonderful Story of Henry Sugar" (thought this was lame as a kid, but I was wrong)</span>
  <a data-text-id="10148538867085773328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer512703381" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17286932150551534767">this was more of a "4 1/2 stars" kind of book, but Dahl is so darned entertaining that i have no problem rounding up. while the final few stories were a bit of a disappointment, they were only a let down because the great majority of these stories are compulsively readable and incredibly satisfying. dahl was a writer who clearly loved telling a good story, and when you read this collection there's no escaping the feeling that dahl had immense fun when wrote these stories. that delight is infecti</span>
  <span id="freeText17286932150551534767" style="display:none">this was more of a "4 1/2 stars" kind of book, but Dahl is so darned entertaining that i have no problem rounding up. while the final few stories were a bit of a disappointment, they were only a let down because the great majority of these stories are compulsively readable and incredibly satisfying. dahl was a writer who clearly loved telling a good story, and when you read this collection there's no escaping the feeling that dahl had immense fun when wrote these stories. that delight is infectious. it has the power to move just about any one who enjoys reading fiction. this collection also serves as a fascinating study in the evolution of a writer. dahl's craft and perspective grows and shifts over the course of the collection such that the writer who penned the first story is in many ways not the one who penned the last. as i've said before, if you think you don't like short stories, try to reading some dahl. if he can't make a convert out of you, probably no one can. highly recommended. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17286932150551534767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_1770901336'><a id="like_count_review_1770901336" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1770901336?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F6330.The_Best_of_Roald_Dahl">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_1738261392" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1738261392" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7355183-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325883579p2/7355183.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1738261392?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/7355183-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1738261392" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer481933470117856846">I wish I could give this 4.5 stars. I hadn't read short stories in a long while, and was reminded of the unique joy of these little single-serving packages of fiction — particularly the first batch of these short stories. I have to say I particularly enjoyed reading these macabre stories full of sex, sin and death while simultaneously reading his more famous books to my children. <br/>These stories are really about the final twist — which is often shocking and brilliant. Having said that, the lead-up</span>
  <span id="freeText481933470117856846" style="display:none">I wish I could give this 4.5 stars. I hadn't read short stories in a long while, and was reminded of the unique joy of these little single-serving packages of fiction — particularly the first batch of these short stories. I have to say I particularly enjoyed reading these macabre stories full of sex, sin and death while simultaneously reading his more famous books to my children. <br />These stories are really about the final twist — which is often shocking and brilliant. Having said that, the lead-up to these twists gets overly long in many cases, particularly in the later stories (the book is organized chronologically). Still, the first half of this book is full of little gems — some of the best and most twisted short stories you could ever hope to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="481933470117856846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

In 1953 he published the best-selling story collection Someone Like You and married actress Patricia Neal. He published the popular book James and the Giant Peach in 1961. In 1964 he released another highly successful work, Charlie and the Chocolate Factory, which was later adapted for two films.</span>

<div id="review_3358633682" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3358633682" />
    <a title="Emilie Marshall" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/103275991-emilie-marshall"><img alt="Emilie Marshall" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569807212p2/103275991.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3358633682?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emilie Marshall" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emilie Marshall" href="/user/show/103275991-emilie-marshall">Emilie Marshall</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3358633682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15555086267249557288">Did you even know Roald Dahl wrote for ADULTS as well as children?? That makes it sound like they're explicit or something--it's not that. "Oh, so they're just not silly and cartoony?" you might ask. No, they can be QUITE silly and cartoony. And at times, very cringeworthy. The emotional rollercoaster Roald Dahl takes you on can make you squirm, squeal, and audibly holler "NOO!!". There are certainly some short stories that aren't terribly terrific, but the terrific ones make up for it tenfold. </span>
  <span id="freeText15555086267249557288" style="display:none">Did you even know Roald Dahl wrote for ADULTS as well as children?? That makes it sound like they're explicit or something--it's not that. "Oh, so they're just not silly and cartoony?" you might ask. No, they can be QUITE silly and cartoony. And at times, very cringeworthy. The emotional rollercoaster Roald Dahl takes you on can make you squirm, squeal, and audibly holler "NOO!!". There are certainly some short stories that aren't terribly terrific, but the terrific ones make up for it tenfold. There's one where a lady murders her good-fer-nuthin husband with a frozen lamb shank, and then cooks it and feeds it to the detectives that are looking for the murder weapon and they're all like "I mean its gotta be around here somewhere. It's probably right under our noses" and the reader chuckles painfully like "BRO. LITERALLY IT IS!" Reading Roald Dahl short stories is like staring wide-eyed at a horror film where you know the consequences of everyone's bad decisions and you beg and scream at them to NOT make those bad decisions as they DO and popcorn is flying everywhere. That is very much the feeling.<br />That makes it sound like they are thrillers, which is not necessarily the case. There's one where two strangers start talking at a country club pool, and one guy casually mentions how his lighter is totally reliable, so the other guy bets him that it can't be lit like...ten times in a row or something without fail. The wager is: Mysterious gambler's gorgeous expensive car to the Lighter-Owner's PINKY. FINGER. Huh?? What a weird bet! But this silver-tongued gambler coerces the fella into it, and the reader is short of breath and their heart is racing as they count down the lighter's attempts....<br /><br />Another one I loved, some guy made a device that hears vibrations and sounds beyond the capacity of human ears. And what does he hear? Why... the agonizing screams of a tree being chopped down!! Among other things. Frantic drama ensues.<br /><br />A couple of blokes start some sort of "revenge" company where you pay them to seek revenge on an enemy. Punches in the eye, putting them in an embarrassing situation, and other sorts of punishments vary in price.<br /><br />An older woman has been having an affair for years, meeting this man twice a year, and when the man decided to cut ties he buys his sweetheart a parting gift of a luxurious and terribly expensive mink coat. Well, shit. She WANTS that damn coat, but if her husband sees her wearing it... he's gonna be like "Umm yeah where did you get that coat that we could never afford I'm onto you lady" so she comes up with an elaborate plan involving a pawn shop and my gosh... of course things don't go to plan. The rising action of that tale comes to such a hilarious crash. <br /><br />So yes, if you loved Charlie and the Chocolate Factory and Matilda etc, then for sure check yourself out some Roald Dahl short stories. <br />So so many good ones!! I've bought copies of this book and other collections of Roald Dahl's short stories and gave them away over and over. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15555086267249557288" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1853858866" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10315373537648821739">I will be updating this review after each story I read in this collection. I haven't read Roald Dahl's work except for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39988.Matilda' title='Matilda by Roald Dahl' rel='nofollow noopener'>Matilda</a> which I loved. I will hide major spoilers but I cannot review the stories without talking on some important things that others might consider as spoilers. So you are warned, spoiler-allergic people!<br/><br/><b>1. Madame Rosetta</b> [<i>Rating: 2.5/5</i>]<br/>This story was <i>just okay</i>. It is about a couple of military pilots who finally get to take some time off in Cairo, Egypt and they plan to relax</span>
  <span id="freeText10315373537648821739" style="display:none">I will be updating this review after each story I read in this collection. I haven't read Roald Dahl's work except for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39988.Matilda" title="Matilda by Roald Dahl" rel="nofollow noopener">Matilda</a> which I loved. I will hide major spoilers but I cannot review the stories without talking on some important things that others might consider as spoilers. So you are warned, spoiler-allergic people!<br /><br /><b>1. Madame Rosetta</b> [<i>Rating: 2.5/5</i>]<br />This story was <i>just okay</i>. It is about a couple of military pilots who finally get to take some time off in Cairo, Egypt and they plan to relax by drinking all night. But one thing leads to an another and they go off on a small adventure that sorta ends with <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ each one getting a girl for the night........I guess? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I particularly like the idea of rescuing the damsel(s) in distress, but the lead male characters(the writer too) objectify women and the whole enterprise these characters undertake, which actually is a very noble thing in general, becomes a hypocrisy of sorts after realizing how it all ends. Of course, when we look at the timeline of the story, this sort of sexism from the characters' part can be written of as only true to reality.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> There is also a very interesting conversation these characters have with a few local Egyptians which I think is a very good example of how humans in general instinctively stereotype others who have views/beliefs/preferences different from their own and cannot quite find the logic or reason that they believe others see which makes them hold their weird/outlandish views/beliefs/preferences in question. <br /><br /><b>2. Man From The South</b> [<i>Rating: 5/5</i>]<br />I loved this short story. It was a breeze to read. It's a 10 page thriller of sorts, just that all the tension is built up on a very stupid, silly premise. And I love that. <br /><br /><b>3. The Sound Machine</b> [<i>Rating: 5/5</i>]<br />Absolutely amazing! It's a little sci-fi-esque story. The ramifications of the discovery made in the story will be unfathomable if it happened in real life. This is a story for <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[vegans <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br /><b>4. Taste</b> [<i>Rating: 3.5/5</i>]<br />Another short about how men make stupid bets. This is in a way similar to the 3rd story.<br /><br /><b>5. Dip In The Pool</b> [<i>Rating: 5/5]</i>]<br />Hahaha!! This was funny, very well written. What a foolish man Mr. Botibol is. Hahahahaa!!<br /><br /><b> 6. Skin</b> [<i>Rating: 5/5]</i>]<br />What could possibly go wrong in getting a tattoo of your wife's face on your back, from neck to waist, by a young painter who is struggling to make a living? I didn't know tattooing existed even at the time of World War 1(or is it just fiction?). I loved the story and the ending.</span>

<div id="review_1997950244" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1997950244" />
    <a title="Nikhilraj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13994655-nikhilraj"><img alt="Nikhilraj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585589445p2/13994655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1997950244?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nikhilraj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nikhilraj" href="/user/show/13994655-nikhilraj">Nikhilraj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/18585177-the-best-of-roald-dahl">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1997950244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12416353873745389719">I came across this book when I was browsing casually through the shelves of the college library. It didn't strike me as an interesting one at first. I didn't recognize the author by name, but when I turned to the back cover, he was introduced as the creator of Charlie and the chocolate factory. I was rather sceptical about Roal Dahl and was a prejudist about his works. I thought they were mostly children's stories and wouldn't have anything that could interest me. But after 30minutes long search</span>
  <span id="freeText12416353873745389719" style="display:none">I came across this book when I was browsing casually through the shelves of the college library. It didn't strike me as an interesting one at first. I didn't recognize the author by name, but when I turned to the back cover, he was introduced as the creator of Charlie and the chocolate factory. I was rather sceptical about Roal Dahl and was a prejudist about his works. I thought they were mostly children's stories and wouldn't have anything that could interest me. But after 30minutes long search I finally settled for this book and a one by Feynman. Ah. . ! But I was wrong to the core. This is one of the most beautifully written set of short stories I have ever read. They are mysterious, thrilling, and have twists and turns that defy everything the story has started with. When I opened the goodreads page of the book, I wasn't surprised to see a rating of above 4. The book deserves it. And this experience literally shattered my prejudism about authors and books. A great reading experience. Must read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12416353873745389719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_6326193" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6326193" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/350659-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1237397600p2/350659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6326193?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/350659-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6326193" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2945665901027622317">Adult stories by Roald Dahl... as opposed to his kids stories that I might enjoy more as an adult than I did then. This is a great collection. Ashley, a friend of mine used to read these aloud when we were camping in Colorado. I have an amusing memory of a group of adults sitting around a fire listening to one. She said she was tired and could not finish the one she was on. And there was revolt- "No you have to!!!" -like a bunch of kids. heh heh. A must have.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_16238633" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16238633" />
    <a title="Beth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/926519-beth"><img alt="Beth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415406681p2/926519.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16238633?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth" href="/user/show/926519-beth">Beth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/926519-beth?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16238633" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer557942603350164956">Though many probably associate Dahl with his children's books (James and the Giant Peach, Charlie and the Chocolate Factory), these very adult, often dark, short stories are a true delight. It's no wonder that Hitchock used "A Dip in the Pool" for a "Hitchcock Presents" episode. Each story comes with an O'Henry-esque punch to it at the end that left me chuckling and/or surprised. I find these stories fun to read aloud during roadtrips - a refreshing break from music. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_16238633'><a id="like_count_review_16238633" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/16238633?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F6330.The_Best_of_Roald_Dahl">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_86061052" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/86061052" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1527615-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/86061052?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/1527615-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86061052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1081942828485752646">The first three quarters of this short story collection were awesome. Dahl is gleefully wicked and fun to read. The last third I couldn't quite make it through. The later stories seemed less subtle, more forced. I knew reading them that something awful was probably coming, but instead of making me want to know what it was, they just made me squirm. The first part is well worth the purchase though. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

My favorite stories was about the boy and the turtle and the sound machine. <br/><br/>The cons: Some chapters made me feel super uncomfortable, very akin to Larry David’s sense of humor and Dahl uses questionable language to describe some of his characters. Also- as a former vegetarian it was hard to read the Vegetarian short story.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('46chGVObz7E2KNc1dnsLdlC+qL3Ik8JHPdzBGiapBOq2oyErsYJUiiUtbGWG61yISTDuimkn8+Yxc9wkL624Zw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ypapc4VCK6z+4zyirocvznIowFo4fWOP2ep5QAhRi303kqlBZ1uwl+3mh/JeF3gwa6aGbZnJUi7VRWR+AVU38A==')})
  }
//]]>

Abdul Kalam had a great belief in the power of the youth. He met over 21 million children and young people in India and outside and spoke to them about the power of knowledge, ambition, moral behaviour and the need to bring about change in society. He travelled to almost every corner of the country meeting the youth in schools, universities and institutions and interacted with them like a committed teacher.In Learning How to Fly some of his nearly 2,000 lectures have been compiled. These lectures were addressed to teachers and students in school and beyond. In each one of them he has spoken about preparing oneself best for life, to identify and overcome challenges and how to bring out the best within each individual. Through stories from his own life, those of his teachers and mentors as well as stories of some of the greatest men and women of the world, and the latest developments in science and technology, he shows us the importance of dreams and the hard work needed to turn those dreams into reality.Filled with warmth, inspiration and a positive attitude, Learning How to Fly is essential reading for every Indian, young and old.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9109051277334083952\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div The years of Cromwell\'s rise to power, shaped by a decade-long civil war, saw a sustained attempt at the collective government of England; the first attempts at a real Union of Britain; the beginnings of empire; a radically new solution to the idea of a national religion; atrocities in Ireland; and the readmission to England of the Jews, a people officially banned for over three and a half centuries. At the end of it, Oliver Cromwell had emerged as the country\'s sole ruler: to his enemies, and probably to most of his countrymen, his legacy looked as likely to last as that of the Stuart dynasty he       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4273.Roald_Dahl">About Roald Dahl</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4273.Roald_Dahl"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1311554908p3/4273.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4273.Roald_Dahl">Roald Dahl
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10768370038961458607">Roald Dahl was a British novelist, short story writer and screenwriter of Norwegian descent, who rose to prominence in the 1940's with works for both children and adults, and became one of the world's bestselling authors.<br/><br/>Dahl's first published work, inspired by a meeting with C. S. Forester, was <i>Shot Down Over Libya</i>. Today the story is published as <i>A Piece of Cake</i>. The story, about his wartime adv</span>
  <span id="freeText10768370038961458607" style="display:none">Roald Dahl was a British novelist, short story writer and screenwriter of Norwegian descent, who rose to prominence in the 1940's with works for both children and adults, and became one of the world's bestselling authors.<br /><br />Dahl's first published work, inspired by a meeting with C. S. Forester, was <i>Shot Down Over Libya</i>. Today the story is published as <i>A Piece of Cake</i>. The story, about his wartime adventures, was bought by the Saturday Evening Post for $900, and propelled him into a career as a writer. Its title was inspired by a highly inaccurate and sensationalized article about the crash that blinded him, which claimed he had been shot down instead of simply having to land because of low fuel.<br /><br />His first children's book was <i>The Gremlins</i>, about mischievous little creatures that were part of RAF folklore. The book was commissioned by Walt Disney for a film that was never made, and published in 1943. Dahl went on to create some of the best-loved children's stories of the 20th century, such as <i>Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</i>, <i>Matilda</i> and <i>James and the Giant Peach</i>.<br /><br />He also had a successful parallel career as the writer of macabre adult short stories, usually with a dark sense of humour and a surprise ending. Many were originally written for American magazines such as <i>Ladies Home Journal</i>, <i>Harper's</i>, <i>Playboy</i> and <i>The New Yorker</i>, then subsequently collected by Dahl into anthologies, gaining world-wide acclaim. Dahl wrote more than 60 short stories and they have appeared in numerous collections, some only being published in book form after his death. His stories also brought him three Edgar Awards: in 1954, for the collection <i>Someone Like You</i>; in 1959, for the story "The Landlady"; and in 1980, for the episode of <i>Tales of the Unexpected</i> based on "Skin".</span>
  <a data-text-id="10768370038961458607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
